During the sodiation, the transition metal (TM) cations reduce to the metallic state, and the respective anions react with the sodium ions. As a result, the metal particles are surrounded by the matrix of the insulating sodium compound, resulting in loss of electrical contact among the TM particles. Here, an amorphous molybdenum sulfide (a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>) electrode is made highly reversible by suppressing TM particle growth via elongating the cation diffusion pathway. Because of the long distance among Mo atoms in a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>, the growth of Mo nuclei is limited. This leads to more frequent nucleation and formation of smaller particles (3–5 nm in diameter). Since the smaller particles have a larger surface area than the bigger particles, the electrical contacts among Mo particles are clearly retained. The a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> anode for NIBs demonstrates a high capacity and excellent cycling retention. This work establishes that the amorphous structure enhances the reversibility and cycling stability of conversion-reaction-based electrodes by elongating the diffusion pathway of the metal ions.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D22721%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DTae-Hee%2BKim%252C%2BJaeWook%2BShin%252C%2BKug-Seung%2BLee%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D20%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b22452%26title%3DOrigin%2Bof%2Bthe%2BSuperior%2BElectrochemical%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BAmorphous-Phase%2BConversion-Reaction-Based%2BElectrode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BFormation%2Bof%2Ba%2BBicontinuous%2BMetal%2BNetwork%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D22729%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions"> Ions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials"> Materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290797&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Metals"> Metals </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes"> Physical and chemical processes </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-ion%20batteries">Na-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Conversion+Reaction&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_conversion%20reaction">conversion reaction</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nucleation+And+Growth&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nucleation%20and%20growth">nucleation and growth</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Amorphous&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_amorphous">amorphous</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Molybdenum+Sulfide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_molybdenum%20sulfide">molybdenum sulfide</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Rechargeable Na-ion batteries (NIBs) have attracted great attention as a promising alternative for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to the earth-abundant and low-cost Na resource. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> In particular, NIBs can be applied for large-scale energy storage for renewable energy sources coupled with solar or wind energies. However, the larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> as compared to that of Li<sup>+</sup> results in more sluggish diffusion kinetics and more serious damage to the structure of the electrode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> These cause low capacity, poor rate capability, and unsatisfactory cycling stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 7 to 9">(7−9)</a> These disadvantages hinder NIBs from practical applications, and therefore, it is crucial to search for suitable Na<sup>+</sup> storage materials with high performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">As a candidate for the high-capacity NIB anodes, conversion-reaction-based electrode materials, such as transition metal (TM) oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a> sulfides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 5, 12 and 13">(5,12,13)</a> and phosphides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 14 to 16">(14−16)</a> have been investigated. Similar to the conversion reaction with the Li, all possible valence states of TM in the materials can be utilized to enable a multielectron redox reaction per TM, which leads to a high specific capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">However, such a high-specific-capacity reaction comes with challenges. During sodiation, desodiation, and overall cycling, the active material undergoes significant changes that result in a detriment to the battery performance. First, during sodiation, the TM cations reduce to the metallic state, and the respective anions react with the sodium ions. As a result, the metal particles are surrounded by the matrix of the insulating sodium compound, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17−19)</a> which leads to loss of electrical contact among the metal particles. Second, during desodiation, the metal particles oxidize and react with the surrounding anions to return to the pristine state. However, ionization of metal particles often leads to the dissolution of the TM and the loss of the active material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 to 22">(20−22)</a> Last, due to the large amount of reaction, the active material particles go through high strain and stress from volume changes throughout cycles. This strain and stress results in particle pulverization and loss of electrical contact. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To overcome the volume change issue, researchers have been widely exploring nanostructured materials to relieve strain and stress during cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 16, 25 and 26">(16,25,26)</a> However, the large surface area of the nanostructures aggravates dissolution of transition metal cations during desodiation. The dissolution problem is often overcome by incorporating metal oxides in the electrode because they can anchor the dissociated TM ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 20, 27 and 28">(20,27,28)</a> Nevertheless, the electrical contact loss via the insulating matrix during sodiation is difficult to overcome since it is governed by intrinsic nucleation and growth properties of the TM. For instance, although FeF<sub>2</sub> and CuF<sub>2</sub> store Li ions through a similar overall reaction pathway, TMF<sub>2</sub> + 2Li<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>–</sup> → 2LiF + TM, the FeF<sub>2</sub> electrode shows much better reversibility and higher cycling stability than the CuF<sub>2</sub> electrode owing to the lower diffusivity of Fe than that of Cu. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 19 and 29 to 31">(19,29−31)</a> Because of the higher diffusivity of Cu ions, Cu metal nuclei formed during lithiation grow more rapidly, resulting in larger Cu particles which are electrically disconnected by the insulating LiF matrix. In contrast, the growth of Fe metal nuclei is more limited, leading to more frequent nucleation and the formation of smaller Fe metal particles with bicontinuous form. The bicontinuous Fe metal particles provide a more effective pathway for local electron transport, which is the origin of the higher reversibility and cycling stability of the FeF<sub>2</sub> electrode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Although the better performance of Fe-based conversion materials is clear, a universal solution to the intrinsic nucleation and growth properties is desired because, otherwise, the options for the choice of materials are severely limited.</div><div class="NLM_p">In this work, as a novel way to bypass the intrinsic issues, we propose to amorphize the active material. By amorphizing the active material, the average interatomic distance between TM ions increases, and hence, we can achieve an elongated TM ion diffusion pathway. Thus, the growth of TM particles is constrained during the sodiation process, leading to more frequent nucleation and smaller particle sizes. Because smaller particles have higher surface area, this will result in more electrical contacts among TM particles.</div><div class="NLM_p last">To demonstrate the validity and applicability of the above strategy, we present an amorphous-phase molybdenum sulfide (a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>) anode via a simple and low-cost electrodeposition method and clarify the origin of improved cyclability and reversibility of the amorphous materials in terms of nucleation and growth of the TM particles. Compared to the crystalline MoS<sub>2</sub>, the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> anode for NIBs demonstrates a high capacity and excellent cycling retention. In addition to the electrochemical performance measurements, the reaction mechanism of the electrode is thoroughly investigated by characterizing the size and distribution of Mo metal particles after sodiation. Compared to the crystalline-phase counterpart, the Mo metal particles are smaller and better dispersed with clear electrical contacts among particles.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> was prepared by galvanostatic electrodeposition at −100 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for 3 min from an aqueous solution containing 0.5 M Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, 1 M Na<sub>2</sub>S·<i>x</i>H<sub>2</sub>O, and 1.4 M H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>. The electrodeposition step was conducted in a two-electrode cell with a nodular Cu foil as a working electrode and a Pt mesh as a counter electrode. The distance between the working electrode and the counter electrode was 1.5 cm, and the electrodeposition was performed at 25 °C with magnetic stirring at 500 rpm. The electrodeposit was rinsed with distilled water and then dried in a vacuum overnight at room temperature. Electrodeposition of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> on commercial 3D Ni foam was conducted by potentiostatic electrodeposition at −8 V (vs SCE) for 30 min. The Ni foam used in this work has purity of 99.5%, pore size of ∼450 μm, and thickness of ∼500 μm. Before electrodeposition, the Ni foam was roll-pressed to a thickness of ∼200 μm.</div><div class="NLM_p">In an electrolytic bath containing molybdate and sulfur ions, the chemical conversion of molybdate to thiomolybdate ions occurs by the following reaction: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/medium/am9b22452_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i4"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">Once the cathodic current density is applied to the Cu substrate in the electrolytic bath, the following reduction reaction of thiomolybdate ions (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>) occurs, resulting in the formation of molybdenum sulfide.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/medium/am9b22452_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">The slurry for a crystalline MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode (c-MoS<sub>2</sub>), which was used for comparison, was fabricated by mixing 70 wt % commercial MoS<sub>2</sub> powder, 20 wt % of a conductive agent (acetylene black), and 10 wt % polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) in a <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) solution. The slurry was pasted onto a Cu foil substrate, followed by vacuum drying at 80 °C overnight.</div><div class="NLM_p">The surface morphology was observed using scanning electron microscopy (SEM). Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) was carried out to investigate the chemical composition of the electrodeposit. The crystal structure of the electrodeposit was investigated by X-ray diffraction (XRD) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM). Samples for the HRTEM analysis conducted in this study were prepared to be uniformly thin to enable the analyzing beam of electrons to penetrate using a focused ion beam (FIB). An oxidation state analysis of Mo was conducted by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) on the surface of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode.</div><div class="NLM_p">X-ray absorption fine structure (XAFS) measurements were carried out on the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and c-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes. All XAFS measurements were performed using the Pohang Accelerator Laboratory (PAL) 8C beamline. All the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> samples (pristine, sodiated, and desodiated) are prepared on a current collector, and the pristine c-MoS<sub>2</sub> is prepared in powder. The crystal structural parameters for MoS<sub>2</sub> and MoO<sub>2</sub> are obtained from the reported values in the literature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 33 to 35">(33−35)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode was investigated using a CR2032 coin-type cell assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox. Each cell consisted of an a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode sheet (with an area of 1 cm<sup>2</sup>) and a Na metal film (with an area of 1 cm<sup>2</sup>) with a glass fiber membrane. Loading of the active material was 0.6–0.7 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The electrolyte was anhydrous propylene carbonate (PC) with 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> and 2 vol % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). The charge/discharge characteristics of the electrode were examined galvanostatically at a current density of 100 mA&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup> over the voltage range 0.001–3.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Differential capacity–voltage (d<i>q</i>/d<i>V</i>) plots were obtained at the initial cycle, with a voltage interval of 4 mV. For galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements, the cells were discharged or charged at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 20 and 15 min for a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and c-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes, respectively, and then rested for 5 h. The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurement was conducted at the 100th sodiated electrodes. All the EIS measurements were obtained with open circuit voltage with 10 mV voltage amplitude. The frequency range was from 100 kHz to 0.5 Hz.</div><div class="NLM_p last">After the first discharge, and after the first discharge–charge cycle, the electrodes were extracted from the coin-type cells for XAFS and XRD measurements. The electrodes were rinsed with anhydrous diethyl carbonate (DEC) and then sealed using Kapton tape.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b shows the surface morphology and a cross-sectional image of the electrodeposit prepared by galvanostatic electrodeposition on a nodular Cu substrate (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, the electrodeposit consists of dense plates with a lateral size of 10–15 μm. The cross-sectional SEM image was obtained using FIB. Platinum and carbon layers were deposited onto the surface of the electrodeposit in order to protect the material from damages by the ion beam and prevent the sample from being destructed during lift-off and mounting processes for TEM sample preparation. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b shows that the electrodeposit has a thickness of 2.6 ± 0.2 μm and is well contacted to the rough Cu substrate. According to the EDS analysis, the atomic contents of Mo and S are 36.5% and 63.5%, respectively. In order to investigate the crystallographic property of the electrodeposit, an HRTEM image and the selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern of the electrodeposit were obtained. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c, lattice fringes are not observed in the HRTEM image, nor are there any diffraction spots or rings in the SAED pattern (inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c). In the XRD pattern of the electrodeposit (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d), only the peaks from the Cu substrate are observed. Moreover, in the zoomed-in XRD pattern, no peaks other than Cu peaks are observed also (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). Because the XRD patterns have been significantly zoomed in, the Kβ, W Lα peaks are observed as well as the Kα peaks. The tungsten (W) line appears because the tungsten filament equipped in the X-ray source can contaminate the target anode and serve as an additional X-ray source. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> All of these results indicate that the electrodeposit has an amorphous structure. Thus, the electrodeposit can be considered as an amorphous form of MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> (a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/medium/am9b22452_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Surface morphology, (b) cross-sectional image, (c) high-resolution TEM image (inset: SAED pattern), and (d) XRD pattern of the electrodeposit prepared by galvanostatic electrodeposition at −100 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for 3 min from an electrolytic bath containing 0.5 M Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, 1 M Na<sub>2</sub>S·<i>x</i>H<sub>2</sub>O, and 1.4 M H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> on a nodular Cu substrate</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22452&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To determine the oxidation state of Mo in the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>, XPS was carried out. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, the Mo 3d spectrum is fitted to three sets of doublet peaks at 229.0 and 232.1 eV (Mo<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> 230.4 and 233.7 eV (Mo<sup>5+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo<sup>5+</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> and 232.5 and 235.5 eV (Mo<sup>6+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo<sup>6+</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref41" aria-label="reference 38 and 41">(38,41)</a> Their measured contents were found to be 43.0%, 35.0%, and 22.0%, respectively. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, the S 2p spectrum shows only one set of doublet peaks at 161.9 and 163.2 eV, corresponding to the 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub> doublets of MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The presence of high-valence-state cations, Mo<sup>5+</sup> and Mo<sup>6+</sup>, with high contents in the XPS result suggests that the surface of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrodeposit is oxidized to Mo<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and MoO<sub>3</sub>.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/medium/am9b22452_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. X-ray photoluminescence spectroscopy (XPS) analysis of (a) the Mo and (b) the S binding energy of the electrodeposit. (c) Experimental χ(<i>R</i>) versus <i>R</i> spectra of a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> (fluorescence mode) and c-MoS<sub>2</sub> powder (transmission mode) before cycling.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22452&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To obtain information pertaining to the bulk electronic structure, an EXAFS analysis of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode and commercial c-MoS<sub>2</sub> was carried out (SEM image and XRD pattern of the commercial c-MoS<sub>2</sub> powder are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c shows the observed EXAFS spectra of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and the c-MoS<sub>2</sub>. In both EXAFS spectra of a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and c-MoS<sub>2</sub>, a distinct peak at 1.8–1.9 Å is observed: at 1.84 Å in the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and at 1.90 Å in the c-MoS<sub>2</sub>. The slightly lower <i>R</i> value of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> can be attributed to the presence of the Mo–O bond. According to the literature, the peak at 1.90 Å corresponds to the Mo–S single scattering path of c-MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The Mo–O bond in the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> has a lower <i>R</i> than the Mo–S bond in the c-MoS<sub>2</sub>, resulting in the shift of the peak to a lower <i>R</i> value. As described earlier, oxygenation of conversion-reaction-based materials often leads to improved cycling performance by suppressing cation and anion dissolution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">It should be noted that the second neighboring Mo–Mo SS peak is nearly absent in the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>, whereas a significant peak at ∼3.0 Å corresponding to the Mo–Mo SS path is clearly observed in the c-MoS<sub>2</sub>, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c. These findings indicate that the Mo atoms in the c-MoS<sub>2</sub> are arranged in an orderly manner with a fixed distance among the nearest Mo atoms. In contrast, in the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>, the Mo atoms are distributed irregularly, and the average distance between the Mo atoms is elongated compared to that for c-MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 45 to 48">(45−48)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance capabilities of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode and the c-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode were investigated. On the basis of the half-cell reaction in this study, sodiation into the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode is referred to as a discharge, and desodiation from the electrode is referred to as a charge. The c-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode shows three distinct plateaus and peak at ∼0.9, ∼0.8, and ∼0.1 V, typical of c-MoS<sub>2</sub>, as shown in the initial discharge curve in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b and d<i>q</i>/d<i>V</i> plot in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. The reaction at ∼0.9 V is known to be an intercalation reaction, and the following reaction at ∼0.8 V is known to be a conversion reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The reaction at ∼0.1 V is most likely associated with an alloying reaction, as determined by the excessive gravimetric capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode does not exhibit any distinct plateau in the initial discharge curve (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). In addition, the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode presents only indistinct d<i>q</i>/d<i>V</i> peaks at around 0.8 and 0.4 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c), implying that most of the sodiation reaction occurs in a pseudocapacitive manner. The crystalline materials have well-defined crystal structure, implying that Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites are electrochemically equivalent to one another. Contrarily, amorphous materials generally have randomly ordered atoms in the structure and a high population of defect sites. Therefore, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites are not discrete and electrochemically nonequivalent, leading to a sloping charge/discharge voltage profile in the whole potential range. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref52 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51 ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 51 to 53">(51−53)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/medium/am9b22452_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Charge–discharge curves of the (a) a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and (b) c-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes upon the first, second, and third cycles. Differential capacity (d<i>q</i>/d<i>V</i>) plots of (c) a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and (d) c-MoS<sub>2</sub>&nbsp;at the first cycle. (e) Cycle performance and Coulombic efficiency of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and c-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> over the voltage range 0.001–3.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). (f) Specific capacities of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode with current density levels cycling from 100 to 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and then back to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22452&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> on the Cu substrate delivers a high charge capacity of 584.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with high Coulombic efficiency of 88.9% upon the first cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). The charge capacity of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode maintains a high value of 487.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> upon the 100th cycle, and the Coulombic efficiency increases gradually during the first few cycles, reaches 97.6% at the fifth cycle, and then maintains a rate of 98.5% for the next 95 cycles. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f, even at high discharge/charge rates, the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode shows a superb electrochemical performance; at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode retains a charge capacity exceeding 520 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, while at 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, it delivered a rate of more than 300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with high reversibility. When the current density returned to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity was restored to approximately 520 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. At a high current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> shows a high cycling stability to 200 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The charge capacity of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode maintains a high value of 354.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> upon the 200th cycle, corresponding to 77.4% retention of the second cycle. The outstanding cycling stability, excellent reversibility, and superb rate capability of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode are highly notable despite the fact that the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode has a microsized structure and does not contain any conductive agent. Contrary to the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode, the c-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode shows poor cycling stability and low reversibility at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; it had a low Coulombic efficiency of 74.7% upon the first cycle, and the charge capacity of c-MoS<sub>2</sub> dropped from 671 to less than 200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> during the initial 25 cycles, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e. As well as at low current density, the c-MoS<sub>2</sub> shows poor cycling stability at a high current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> as presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. EIS measurements after the 100th sodiation were also conducted on the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and c-MoS<sub>2</sub>. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, a-MoS<sub>2</sub> shows much lower charge transfer resistance than c-MoS<sub>2</sub> after 100 cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to investigate phase transitions in the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode during the discharge and charge processes, XRD and XAFS analyses were conducted. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> shows XRD patterns of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode before cycling, after sodiation, and after desodiation. Before cycling, only the peaks from the Cu substrate are observed. After sodiation and desodiation, only the broad peak at 2θ = ∼17° originating from the Kapton tape and the peaks from the Cu substrate are observed. These results reflect that the products formed through sodiation and desodiation of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> have poor long-range ordering.</div><div class="NLM_p">The XAFS analysis, which investigates the short-range order, provides more direct information about any phase transitions which occur during the electrochemical reaction of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode. Generally in the XANES region, when the oxidation state of the metal increases, the absorption edge shifts toward a higher binding energy. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the Mo K-edge spectra of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode before cycling and after the first sodiation and desodiation processes. The absorption edge of the sample after the first sodiation step shifts toward a lower binding energy and approaches the edge of the Mo metal reference, indicating that the Mo cations are reduced to Mo metal during the sodiation process. After the first desodiation process, the absorption edge of the sample shifts back toward a higher value, indicating that the Mo metal becomes oxidized during the desodiation process.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/medium/am9b22452_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Mo K-edge XANES spectra change of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> during the first cycle. (b) Experimental χ(<i>R</i>) versus <i>R</i> spectra of a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> after the first sodiation and after the first desodiation processes, and the theoretical single scattering paths of Mo–S and Mo–Mo. (The intensity of the Mo–Mo paths was divided by 2.)</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22452&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b shows the observed EXAFS of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode after the first sodiation and desodiation processes along with the theoretical single scattering paths for Mo–S and Mo–Mo. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34 ref54" aria-label="reference 33, 34 and 54">(33,34,54)</a> It is clearly shown that, as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, after the first sodiation step, the intensity of the peak at 1.84 Å (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c) is reduced significantly and moves toward a lower <i>R</i> value (1.69 Å), compared to the sample before cycling. This suggests that the Mo–S bonds are broken during the sodiation process, whereas the Mo–O with low bond length remains. These results imply that the capacity of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> is mainly originated from the Mo–S species, whereas the Mo–O species is inactive. Instead, a new peak emerges at a higher radial distance of ∼2.33 Å, corresponding to the nearest Mo–Mo SS path of the Mo metal. These EXAFS results suggest that the Mo–S bonds in the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode are broken and that metallic Mo–Mo bonds form during the first sodiation process. After desodiation, the intensity of the Mo–S SS peak increases and moves back to a higher <i>R</i> value, whereas the intensity of the Mo–Mo SS peak is significantly reduced. These results demonstrate that Mo–S bonds are reversibly recovered during the desodiation process. On the other hand, Mo–O bonds remain inactive.</div><div class="NLM_p">Here, the sample has two major contributing factors, oxygen involvement and amorphization. To be able to isolate these two factors, we conducted a cycling test after annealing the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> at 300 °C for 6 h under high-purity Ar gas. Although significant XRD peaks are not observed after the annealing process (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>), distinct peaks are observed at 1.59 and 2.05 V in the charged<i>q</i>/d<i>V</i> plot of the annealed a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>). The distinct peaks are not illustrated in the&nbsp;d<i>q</i>/d<i>V</i> plot of the as prepared a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>. These results demonstrate that the annealed a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> has a more ordered structure closer to the c-MoS<sub>2</sub> compared to the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7c</a>, the charge capacity of the annealed a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> gradually decreases with cycling, delivering only 360.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the 20th cycle, 74.0% of the second charge capacity. Moreover, the annealed a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> shows a relatively low initial Coulombic efficiency of 83.3%, whereas the as-prepared a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> shows 92.7%. These results reflect that the crystallinity of the molybdenum sulfide has a great impact on the electrochemical performance rather than presence of the oxide/oxygen in the molybdenum sulfide and that the amorphous structure improves both cyclability and reversibility of the conversion-reaction-based material.</div><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the effect of amorphization on the conversion reaction and the size and distribution of the Mo metal particles which formed after the sodiation process, bright-field TEM (BF TEM) images were taken from cross-sectional areas of fully sodiated a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and c-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). In the BF TEM images, it is observed that dark-contrast particles are surrounded by the light-contrast matrix. In order to confirm the composition of the dark-contrast particles, EDAX elemental mapping on the cross-section is conducted. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> shows the BF TEM image of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and c-MoS<sub>2</sub> after sodiation and the corresponding EDAX elemental mapping images for Mo. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8b,d</a>, the concentrated blue signals of Mo are overlapped with the dark-contrast particles in BF TEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a,c</a>). Based on the EDAX elemental mapping analysis, the dark-contrast particles in the bright-field images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> can be considered as Mo particles. After the first sodiation step for a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>, a large number of Mo particles (dark contrast) formed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a) with a remarkably small size of 3–5 nm in diameter (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b). Owing to the large surface area, there are frequent contacts among the small Mo particles, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. The lighter contrast in the background is most likely the Na<sub>2</sub>S matrix. Contrary to the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> sample, the c-MoS<sub>2</sub> sample generated larger Mo particles with diameters of 10–20 nm and which are fully wrapped in the insulating Na<sub>2</sub>S matrix (light contrast) without significantly visible contacts among the particles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c,d).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/medium/am9b22452_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Morphology and spatial phase distribution of (a, b) the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode and (c, d) the commercial c-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode, respectively, after first sodiation showing bright-field HRTEM images at different magnifications.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22452&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The differences in the sizes and densities of the Mo metal particles between the sodiated samples of a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and c-MoS<sub>2</sub>, as demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>, are attributed to the different distances between the Mo atoms when in the pristine states. During the electrochemical reaction, the density and size of the nuclei are strongly affected by the distance (or concentration) of source ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 55 and 56">(55,56)</a> When metal ions are reduced when they are spaced farther apart, the atoms must travel farther in order to form a nucleus and grow. Generally, the nucleation process is limited by the activation energy, and the growth process is limited by diffusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> Thus, if the diffusion pathway is elongated, the reduction will yield a larger number of smaller nuclei. In the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>, the average distances between Mo atoms are elongated (compared to c-MoS<sub>2</sub>), as suggested by the EXAFS analysis of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). Due to the longer distances between Mo atoms, the growth of Mo nuclei is hindered, leading to more frequent nuclei formation and smaller particle sizes, as depicted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>a. The small-sized particles have a large surface area, resulting in a high probability of contact formation among the particles. In contrast, Mo nuclei can grow more rapidly during the sodiation of c-MoS<sub>2</sub> due to the shorter diffusion pathway of Mo atoms. Consequently, Mo metal grows to a larger size, and they are separated from each other, as presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>b.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/medium/am9b22452_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0007.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Illustration of the Metal Particle Formation Process of (a) the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and (b) the c-MoS<sub>2</sub> Electrodes during the First Sodiation Step (For Convenience of Explanation, Sulfur Atoms Are Not Shown)<sup>a</sup></div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22452&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div><p class="last"></p><p class="last"><span class="fn-label"><sup>a</sup></span>Left: electron transport through connected Mo particles.</p><p></p></figure><div class="NLM_p">The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) result for the first discharge of the c-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a) shows similar equilibrium potentials for the reactions corresponding to ∼0.9 and ∼0.8 V in the first discharge curve. The reaction for the ∼0.9 V plateau, known to be an intercalation reaction, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> shows a very low overpotential. However, the overpotential suddenly increases for the reaction at ∼0.8 V. Notably, the overpotential for the reaction at ∼0.8 V is relieved slightly at a very early stage of the reaction. This large overpotential at the very early stage of the plateau and the subsequent relief of the overpotential are often associated with the activation energy for the nucleation and growth of Mo metal particles, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref57" aria-label="reference 21 and 57">(21,57)</a> In the case of a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>, no distinct plateaus in the voltage profile (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a) nor in the GITT results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b) are observed during the first discharge, and a nearly constant overpotential is applied during the entire discharge process. These outcomes suggest that the conversion reaction occurs throughout the sodiation process, with the growth process significantly suppressed from the start to the end of the discharge process. Compared to c-MoS<sub>2</sub>, a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> undergoes much less growth, enabling formation of the smaller Mo metal particles, as noted in the HRTEM results.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/medium/am9b22452_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. GITT results obtained at the first discharge for (a) the commercial c-MoS<sub>2</sub> and (b) the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/asset/images/large/am9b22452_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22452&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">In order to dramatically increase the loading mass, a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> was electrodeposited onto a commercial 3D Ni foam (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). In the electrode prepared on a 3D substrate (Ni foam in this work), the average electron and ion transfer length through the active material is much shorter than that in the electrode prepared on a conventional current collector (Cu foil in this work); for a given areal loading of active material, the thickness of the active material layer on the metal frame of a 3D substrate is much thinner than that on the current collector. Therefore, higher mass loading of the active material can be achieved by using the 3D substrate, leading to an increase in the energy of the cell without a loss of power density. The increasing areal capacity of rechargeable batteries is one of the crucial means by which to increase the cell-level energy density while simultaneously lowering the cost. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>, the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> on the Ni foam exhibited a remarkable areal charge capacity of 3.5 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> initially. It exceeded 2 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> upon the 100th cycle, thus revealing a highly stable cycling performance. To the best of our knowledge, the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> demonstrates the highest areal capacity among all previously reported MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes as an anode material for Na-ion batteries (the literature values are 0.44–1.21 mA g cm<sup>–2</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref12 ref58 ref59 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref12 ref58 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 5, 12 and 58 to 60">(5,12,58−60)</a></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, the highly reversible a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode was prepared by suppressing TM particle growth via elongating the cation diffusion pathway. Because of the elongated distance between Mo atoms in a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>, the growth of Mo nuclei is limited leading to a more frequent nucleation and formation of smaller particles (3–5 nm in diameter). Since the smaller particles have a larger surface area than the bigger particles, the probability of retaining electrical contact among Mo particles is higher. This is the origin of high-reversibility and long-term stability of the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode. This work establishes that the amorphous structure enhances the reversibility and cycling stability of conversion-reaction-based electrodes by elongating the diffusion pathway of the metal ions. Thus, the amorphous structure can be utilized universally to overcome the particle growth challenge even based on the intrinsic property of the material. Furthermore, by preparing the a-MoS<sub><i>x</i></sub> electrode on a 3D foam substrate using the simple electrodeposition method, the areal capacity of the Na-ion battery anode could be enhanced up to 3.5 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> with a highly stable cycling performance. In this regard, the amorphous-phase electrode is a good choice for the practical use of conversion-reaction-based secondary batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b22452?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b22452</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images, XRD patterns, cycle performance and Coulombic efficiency, EIS plots, differential capacity plots, charge capacity, and charge–discharge curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf">am9b22452_si_001.pdf (1.36 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b22452" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">One-step synthesis of multilayered 2D Sn nanodendrites as a high-performance anode material for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Tae-Hee; Hong, Kyung-Sik; Sohn, DongRak; Kim, MinJoong; Nam, Do-Hwan; Cho, EunAe; Kwon, HyukSang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">38</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">20304-20315</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Multilayered 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites were synthesized by a 1-step electrodeposition process from an aq. soln. contg. coumarin (C9H6O2).  Under a high cathodic c.d. that is necessary for dendritic growth, H bubbles evolved with Sn redn.  The H bubbles limited the diffusion path of the Sn ions to space between H bubbles and concealed the splitting sites for addnl. branches, which suppressed the development of the branches.  The addn. of coumarin greatly reduced the departure diam. of the H bubbles and thus made the growth of the Sn dendrites with a high d. of nanosized branches possible.  Also, coumarin suppressed Sn crystallite growth along the [001] direction, promoting 2-dimensional growth of the 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites along the set of [220] directions that are normal to the [001] direction.  These 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites grew layer-by-layer on a substrate, forming a unique multilayered structure.  As an anode material for Na-ion batteries, the multilayered 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites exhibited a high specific capacity of 783.88 mA h g-1 and excellent cycle performance with 96.6% capacity retention over 300 cycles.  Also, the multilayered 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites showed a superior rate capability of 412.84 mA g-1 at 5C.  The outstanding performance is attributed to a high d. of free space inside the multilayered 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites and the nanosized Sn branches that offer short diffusion paths for Na ions.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/nn505536t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nam, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, T.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, K.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwon, H.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Template-Free Electrochemical Synthesis of Sn Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode Materials for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">11</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">11824</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">11835</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/nn505536t</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D8%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D11824-11835%26journal%3DACS%2BNano%26issue%3D11%26author%3DD.-H.%2BNam%26author%3DT.-H.%2BKim%26author%3DK.-S.%2BHong%26author%3DH.-S.%2BKwon%26title%3DTemplate-Free%2BElectrochemical%2BSynthesis%2Bof%2BSn%2BNanofibers%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fnn505536t&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b22452&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fnn505536t&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Template-Free Electrochemical Synthesis of Sn Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode Materials for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nam, Do-Hwan; Kim, Tae-Hee; Hong, Kyung-Sik; Kwon, Hyuk-Sang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11824-11835</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn nanofibers with a high aspect ratio are successfully synthesized using a simple electrodeposition process from an aq. soln. without the use of templates.  The synthesis approach involves the rapid electrochem. deposition of Sn accompanied by the strong adsorption of Triton X-100, which can function as a growth modifier for the Sn crystallites.  Triton X-100 is adsorbed on the {200} crystallog. planes of Sn in an elongated configuration and suppressed the preferential growth of Sn along the [100] direction.  Consequently, the Sn electrodeposits are forced to grow anisotropically in a direction normal to the (112) or (‾112) plane, forming one-dimensional nanofibers.  As electrode materials for the Na-ion batteries, the Sn nanofibers exhibit a high reversible capacity and an excellent cycle performance; the charge capacity is maintained at 776.26 mA-h/g after 100 cycles, which corresponds to a retention of 95.09% of the initial charge capacity.  The superior electrochem. performance of the Sn nanofibers is mainly attributed to the high mech. stability of the nanofibers, which originate from highly anisotropic expansion during sodiation and the pore vols. existing between the nanofibers.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/jp504055j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nam, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, K.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lim, S.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, T.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwon, H.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Properties of Electrodeposited Sn Anodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Properties of Electrodeposited Sn Anodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nam, Do-Hwan; Hong, Kyung-Sik; Lim, Sung-Jin; Kim, Tae-Hee; Kwon, Hyuk-Sang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1932-7447</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. properties and sodiation/desodiation mechanism of Sn electrodes prepd. by electrodeposition were investigated for Na-ion batteries.  The sodiation of the electrodeposited Sn electrodes proceeded via three conjugated reactions in the cutoff voltage range of 0.001-0.65 V, as follows: the β-Sn phase, which contains a small amt. of Na, was sodiated into amorphous NaSn, and cryst. Na9Sn4 and Na15Sn4 were then formed sequentially.  However, the cycle performance of the electrode was highly dependent on the morphologies and the crystallog. orientations of the electrodeposits.  The Sn electrode composed of coarse particles had the poor cycle performance due to the elec. isolation of Sn caused by the large vol. changes during cycling.  In contrast, the thin-layered Sn electrode exhibited a stable cycle performance in which the charge capacity was maintained at 607.51 mA-h/g after 40 cycles, which corresponds to 98.21% of its initial charge capacity.  The improvement in the cyclability of the layered Sn electrode was attributed not only to reduced structural degrdns. by restricting the cutoff voltage but also to an improved bond between the Sn electrodeposit and the Cu substrate.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b04225"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, K.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nam, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lim, S.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sohn, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, T.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwon, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemically Synthesized Sb/Sb2O3 Composites as High-Capacity Anode Materials Utilizing a Reversible Conversion Reaction for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemically Synthesized Sb/Sb2O3 Composites as High-Capacity Anode Materials Utilizing a Reversible Conversion Reaction for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Kyung-Sik; Nam, Do-Hwan; Lim, Sung-Jin; Sohn, DongRak; Kim, Tae-Hee; Kwon, HyukSang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">31</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">17264-17271</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb/Sb2O3 composites are synthesized by a one-step electrodeposition process from an aq. electrolytic bath contg. a potassium antimony tartrate complex.  The synthesis process involves the electrodeposition of Sb simultaneously with the chem. deposition of Sb2O3, which allows for the direct deposition of morula-like Sb/Sb2O3 particles on the current collector without using a binder.  Structural characterization confirms that the Sb/Sb2O3 composites are composed of approx. 90 mol % metallic Sb and 10 mol % cryst. Sb2O3.  The composite exhibits a high reversible capacity (670 mAh/g) that is higher than the theor. capacity of Sb (660 mAh/g).  The high reversible capacity results from the conversion reaction between Na2O and Sb2O3 that occurs addnl. to the alloying/dealloying reaction of Sb with Na.  Moreover, the Sb/Sb2O3 composite shows excellent cycle performance with 91.8% capacity retention over 100 cycles, and a superior rate capability of 212 mAh/g at a high c.d. of 3300 mA/g.  The outstanding cycle performance is attributed to an amorphous Na2O phase generated by the conversion reaction, which inhibits agglomeration of Sb particles and acts as an effective buffer against vol. change of Sb during cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2017.04.017"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Teng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Świerczek, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">MoS2 Nanosheets Vertically Grown on Reduced Graphene Oxide via Oxygen Bonds with Carbon Coating as Ultrafast Sodium Ion Batteries Anodes</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>119</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">91</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">100</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rational material design is a key to develop high performance electrode for Na ion batteries (SIBs).  Sandwich-like graphene@MoS2@C sheets (G@MS@C), with MoS2 nanosheets perpendicularly connecting with reduced graphene oxide (rGO) through the direct chem. coupling (C-O-Mo bonds), are synthesized by a facile two-step method, which involves in situ growth of MoS2 on rGO sheets and a followed amorphous carbon coverage process.  The interfacial interaction via the C-O-Mo bonds can accelerate electron transport rate and enhance structural stability of G@MS@C electrode.  Meanwhile, the vertical nanostructure provides more active sites and short diffusion distance for sodium ion reaction, leading to fast electrode reaction kinetics.  The rGO sheets and carbon shells not only improve the elec. cond. of the composite, but also act as buffers to accommodate the vol. changes of MoS2 and ensure the integrity of the electrode during cycling.  As an anode material for SIBs, the G@MS@C electrode shows superior reversible capacity (520 mAh/g after 110 cycles at 100 mA/g), excellent rate capability (304 mAh/g after 200 cycles at 5 A/g) and stable cycling performance (260 mAh/g after 300 cycles at 10 A/g).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/nl404165c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kravchyk, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Walter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kovalenko, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report colloidal synthesis of Sb nanocrystals with mean size tunable in the 10-20 nm range and with narrow size distributions of 7-11%.  In comparison to microcryst. Sb, 10 and 20 nm Sb nanocrystals exhibit enhanced rate capability and higher cycling stability as anode materials in rechargeable Li-ion and Na-ion batteries.  All three particle sizes of Sb have high and similar Li-ion and Na-ion charge storage capacities of 580-640 mA-h/g at moderate charging/discharging current densities of 0.5-1C (1C rate is 660 mA/g).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Pure micrometric antimony can be successfully used as neg. electrode material in Na-ion batteries, sustaining a capacity close to 600 mAh g-1 at a high rate with a Coulombic efficiency of 99 over 160 cycles, an extremely high capacity compared to any other compd. tested against both Li and Na.  The reaction mechanism with Na does not simply go through the alloying mechanism obsd. for Li where the intermediate species are those expected from the phase diagram.  In the case of Na, the intermediate phases are mostly amorphous and could not be precisely identified.  Surprisingly, it is evidenced that a competition takes place at the end of the discharge of the Sb/Na cell between the formation of the hexagonal and the cubic polymorphs of Na3Sb, the last being described in the literature as unstable at atm. pressure and only synthesized under high pressure (1-9 GPa).         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions.
