We demonstrated that the Co3O4/CNT nanocomposite is capable of delivering a stable and high capacity of 403 mA h g-1 at 50 mA g-1 after 100 cycles where the sodium uptake/ext. is confirmed in the way of reversible conversion reaction by adopting ex situ techniques.  The rate capability of the composite is significantly improved and its reversible capacity is measured to be 212 mA h g-1 at 1.6 A g-1 and 190 mA h g-1 at 3.2 A g-1, resp.  Due to the simple synthesis technique with high electrochem. performance, Co3O4/CNT nanocomposites have great potential as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA00030H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">2D MoS2/Polyaniline Heterostructures with Enlarged Interlayer Spacing for Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The exploitation of high-capacity and long-life MoS2-based materials is highly important for developing lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  Herein, we demonstrate the confined synthesis of 2D MoS2/polyaniline (MoS2/PANI) nanosheet heterostructures with well-defined interfaces, in which the interlayer distance of MoS2 is greatly enlarged from 0.62 nm to 1.08 nm.  The introduction of such a big interlayer distance for efficient Li+/Na+ storage has never been demonstrated before.  The unique MoS2/PANI nanosheets can address well the key challenges of traditional MoS2 anode materials related to low cond. particularly in the vertical direction, easy restacking/aggregation, large volumetric change and sluggish Li+/Na+ diffusion kinetics in the interlamination. A state-of-the-art ab initio mol. dynamics (AIMD) simulation also reveals that MoS2/PANI nanosheets with enlarged interlayer spacing possess a remarkably improved Li+/Na+ diffusion mobility compared to pristine MoS2 nanosheets.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Half-Cell and Full-Cell Applications of Highly Stable and Binder-Free Sodium Ion Batteries Based on Cu3P Nanowire Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Mouping; Chen, Yu; Xie, Yihao; Yang, Tingzhou; Shen, Xiaowei; Xu, Na; Yu, Haiying; Yan, Chenglin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion battery (SIB) is esp. attractive in cost-effective energy storage device as an alternative to lithium-ion battery.  Particularly, metal phosphides as potential anodes for SIBs have recently been demonstrated owing to their higher specific capacities compared with those of carbonaceous materials.  Unfortunately, most reported metal phosphides consist of irregular particles ranged from several hundreds nanometers to tens of micrometers, thus delivering limited cyclic stability.  This paper reports the sodium storage properties of additive-free Cu3P nanowire (CPNW) anode directly grown on copper current collector via an in situ growth followed by phosphidation method.  Therefore, as a result of its structure features, CPNW anode demonstrates highly stable cycling ability with an ≈70% retention in capacity at the 260th cycle, whereas most reported metal phosphides have limited cycle nos. ranged between 30 and 150.  Besides, the reaction mechanism between Cu3P and Na is investigated by examg. the intermediate products at different charge/discharge stages using ex situ X-ray diffraction measurements.  Furthermore, to explore the practical application of CPNW anode, a pouch-type Na+ full cell consisting of CPNW anode and Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode is assembled and characterized.  As a demonstration, a 10 cm × 10 cm light-emitting diode (LED) screen is successfully powered by the Na+ full cell.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2007.09.084"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dupont, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Laruelle, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grugeon, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dickinson, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Mesoporous Cr2O3 as negative electrode in lithium batteries: TEM study of the texture effect on the polymeric layer formation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dupont, L.; Laruelle, S.; Grugeon, S.; Dickinson, C.; Zhou, W.; Tarascon, J.-M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2008</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">175</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">502-509</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Mesoporous single crystal (PSC) oxides have been reported as presenting higher electrochem. performances than bulk materials in lithium ion batteries operating via intercalation processes.  Here, we extend this study to the electrochem. behavior of mesoporous Cr2O3 vs. Li+/Li0.  We confirm that the Cr2O3 reacts towards Li through a conversion reaction mechanism leading, upon discharge, to the formation of large metallic chromium nanoparticles (10 nm); the latter are embedded into a Li2O matrix together with, in this specific case, a copious amt. of polymeric materials coming from electrolyte degrdn., surrounding the particles, and filling the pores.  During the following charge, re-oxidn. of the nanoparticles occurs with the formation of CrO1-x, with the main difference, as opposed to bulk Cr2O3 electrodes, being the preservation of the polymeric layer at the end of the charge.  We believe the material mesoporosity, via capillary effects, to be at the origin of such a difference.  These electrolyte degrdn. products are shown to help in maintaining the material mesoporosity for a great no. of cycles; and interestingly they are not detrimental to the cell performance in terms of capacity retention while presenting great advantages in terms of charge transfer by reducing diffusion lengths, namely for Li+ ions.  The pos. attributes of mesoporous material-based electrodes noticed for insertion reactions can then be extended to conversion reaction electrodes as long as we can master their synthesis while controlling their mesoporosity through either soft or hard templating techniques.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1149/1.1801451"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Balaya, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maier, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Li-Storage via Heterogeneous Reaction in Selected Binary Metal Fluorides and Oxides</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Conversion Reaction Mechanisms in Lithium Ion Batteries: Study of the Binary Metal Fluoride Electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Feng; Robert, Rosa; Chernova, Natasha A.; Pereira, Nathalie; Omenya, Fredrick; Badway, Fadwa; Hua, Xiao; Ruotolo, Michael; Zhang, Rui-Gang; Wu, Li-Jun; Volkov, Vyacheslav; Su, Dong; Key, Baris; Whittingham, M. Stanley; Grey, Clare P.; Amatucci, Glenn G.; Zhu, Yi-Mei; Graetz, Jason</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">133</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">46</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Materials that undergo a conversion reaction with lithium (e.g., metal fluorides MF2: M = Fe, Cu, ...) often accommodate more than one Li atom per transition-metal cation, and are promising candidates for high-capacity cathodes for lithium ion batteries.  However, little is known about the mechanisms involved in the conversion process, the origins of the large polarization during electrochem. cycling, and why some materials are reversible (e.g., FeF2) while others are not (e.g., CuF2).  The authors studied the conversion reaction of binary metal fluorides, FeF2 and CuF2, using local and bulk probes to better understand the mechanisms underlying their contrasting electrochem. behavior.  X-ray pair-distribution-function and magnetization measurements were used to det. changes in short-range ordering, particle size and microstructure, while high-resoln. TEM and EELS were used to measure the at.-level structure of individual particles and map the phase distribution in the initial and fully lithiated electrodes.  Both FeF2 and CuF2 react with lithium via a direct conversion process with no intercalation step, but there are differences in the conversion process and final phase distribution.  During the reaction of Li+ with FeF2, small metallic iron nanoparticles ( &lt; 5 nm in diam.) nucleate in close proximity to the converted LiF phase, as a result of the low diffusivity of iron.  The iron nanoparticles are interconnected and form a bicontinuous network, which provides a pathway for local electron transport through the insulating LiF phase.  The massive interface formed between nanoscale solid phases provides a pathway for ionic transport during the conversion process.  These results offer the 1st exptl. evidence explaining the origins of the high lithium reversibility in FeF2.  In contrast to FeF2, no continuous Cu network was obsd. in the lithiated CuF2; rather, the converted Cu segregates to large particles (5-12 nm in diam.) during the 1st discharge, which may be partially responsible for the lack of reversibility in the CuF2 electrode.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1007/s12274-016-1365-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, J. In this work, we combine first principles calcns. and electrochem. expts. with characterization tools to probe the conversion reaction voltage of transition metal difluorides MF2 (M = Fe, Ni, and Cu).  We find that the conversion reaction voltage is heavily dependent on the size of the metal nanoparticles generated.  The surface energy of metal nanoparticles appears to penalize the reaction energy, which results in a lower voltage compared to the thermodn. voltage of a bulk-phase reaction.  Furthermore, we develop a reversible CuF2 electrode coated with NiO. This suggests that NiO can be considered a suitable artificial solid electrolyte interphase that prevents direct contact between Cu nanoparticles and the electrolyte.  Thus, it alleviates Cu dissoln. into the electrolyte and improves the reversibility of CuF2.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Conversion materials for lithium ion batteries have recently attracted considerable attention due to their exceptional specific capacities.  Some metal fluorides, such as CuF2, are promising candidates for cathode materials owing to their high operating potential, which stems from the high electronegativity of fluorine.  However, the high ionicity of the metal-fluorine bond leads to a large band gap that renders these materials poor electronic conductors.  Nanosizing the active material and embedding it within a conductive matrix such as carbon can greatly improve its electrochem. performance.  In contrast to other fluorides, such as FeF2 and NiF2, good capacity retention has not, however, been achieved for CuF2.  The reaction mechanisms that occur in the first and subsequent cycles and the reasons for the poor charge performance of CuF2 are studied in this paper via a variety of characterization methods.  In situ pair distribution function anal. clearly shows CuF2 conversion in the first discharge.  However, few structural changes are seen in the following charge and subsequent cycles.  Cyclic voltammetry results, in combination with in situ X-ray absorption near edge structure and ex situ NMR spectroscopy, indicate that Cu dissoln. is assocd. with the consumption of the LiF phase, which occurs during the first charge via the formation of a Cu1+ intermediate.  The dissoln. process consequently prevents Cu and LiF from transforming back to CuF2.  Such side reactions result in negligible capacity in subsequent cycles and make this material challenging to use in a rechargeable battery.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1016/j.mattod.2014.10.040"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nitta, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J. Performance characteristics, current limitations, and recent breakthroughs in the development of com. intercalation materials such as lithium cobalt oxide (LCO), lithium nickel cobalt manganese oxide (NCM), lithium nickel cobalt aluminum oxide (NCA), lithium iron phosphate (LFP), lithium titanium oxide (LTO) and others are contrasted with that of conversion materials, such as alloying anodes (Si, Ge, Sn, etc.), chalcogenides (S, Se, Te), and metal halides (F, Cl, Br, I).                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1039/C6RA24800D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nan, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Facile Synthesis of Ultrathin MoS2/C Nanosheets for Use in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">285</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">289</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Facile synthesis of ultrathin MoS2/C nanosheets for use in sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Minchan; Wu, Zhe; Wang, Zhenyu; Yu, Sicen; Zhu, Yinggang; Nan, Bo; Shi, Yang; Gu, Yingying; Liu, Hongtao; Tang, Yougen; Lu, Zhouguang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">We present a clean and simple method for the synthesis of MoS2/C hybrids. When evaluated as anodes for sodium-ion batteries, the MoS2/C nanocomposite exhibits good electrochem. performance.  The first discharge/charge capacities at c.d. 50 mA g-1 are 784.3 and 590.0 mA h g-1, resp., with initial coulombic efficiency of approx. 75%.  It exhibits superior rate capabilities, with specific discharge capacities of 513.1, 467.3, 437.1, 399.2, 361.8, and 302.7 mA h g-1 at current densities of 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA g-1, resp.  This superior electrochem. performance is mainly due to the synergistic effect between the uniformly-distributed, ultrathin MoS2 nanosheets and the highly graphitized carbon.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Few-layer MoS2 nanosheets are successfully synthesized using a simple and scalable ultrasonic exfoliation technique.                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Deposition of ZnO on bismuth species towards a rechargeable Zn-based aqueous battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shin, JaeWook; You, Jung-Min; Lee, Jungwoo Z.; Kumar, Rajan; Yin, Lu; Wang, Joseph; Shirley Meng, Y.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Zn aq. batteries typically suffer from poor cycle life because water sol. zincate ions are formed during the oxidn. of Zn.  When Zn is oxidized, most of the Zn2+ detach from the current collector and become electrochem. inactive, leaving the battery non-rechargeable.  Numerous reports demonstrate the use of Bi2O3 as an electrode additive to enhance electrochem. performance and they attribute this phenomenon to the improvement in elec. cond.  However, cond. does not have an effect on the intrinsic soly. of the zincate ion.  We conducted a series of characterizations to provide a mechanistic role of Bi2O3 in the Zn electrode.  We find that upon oxidn., zincate ions are formed but they relax into ZnO on the surface of the Bi species.  This work proposes that the reason for the prolonged cycle life is due to the deposition of ZnO through relaxation and this prevents losing electrochem. active materials.  This finding paves the way for further improving the cycle life and understanding the mechanism of the Zn based rechargeable aq. batteries and possibly other conversion types of rechargeable batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201602096"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kumar, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shin, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, J.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Y.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Identifying the Local Structures Formed during Lithiation of the Conversion Material, Iron Fluoride, in a Li Ion Battery: A Solid-State NMR, X-ray Diffraction, and Pair Distribution Function Analysis Study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yamakawa, Naoko; Jiang, Meng; Key, Baris; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The structural transformations that occur when FeF3 is cycled at room temp. in a Li cell were investigated using a combination of x-ray diffraction (XRD), pair distribution function (PDF) anal., and magic-angle-spinning NMR spectroscopy.  Two regions are seen on discharge.  The first occurs between Li = 0 and 1.0 and involves an insertion reaction.  This first region actually comprises two steps.  First, a two-phase reaction between Li = 0 and 0.5 occurs, and the Li0.5FeF3 phase that is formed gives rise to a Li NMR resonance due to Li+ ions near both Fe3+ and Fe2+ ions.  On the basis of the PDF data, the local structure of this phase is closer to the rutile structure than the original ReO3 structure.  Second, a single-phase intercalation reaction occurs between Li = 0.5 and 1.0, for which the Li NMR data indicate a progressive increase in the concn. of Fe2+ ions.  In the second region, the conversion reaction, superparamagnetic, nanosized (∼3 nm) Fe metal is formed, as indicated by the XRD and NMR data, along with some LiF and a third phase that is rich in Li and F.  The charge process involves the formation of a series of intercalation phases with increasing Fe oxidn. state, which, on the basis of the Li NMR and PDF data, have local structures that are similar to the intercalation phases seen during the first stage of the discharge process.  The solid-state NMR and XRD results for the rutile phase FeF2 are presented for comparison, and the data indicate that an insertion reaction also occurs, which is accompanied by the formation of LiF.  This is followed by the formation of Fe nanoparticles and LiF via a conversion reaction.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref31" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.7b11685"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, J. Unlike com. alk. batteries with poor cyclic performance, the nanosized β-MnO2 cathode in the mixt. of LiOH and KOH electrolyte enables rechargeable reactions with high capacity.  To unveil the underlying reaction mechanisms of nanosized β-MnO2, the authors combine thermodn. frameworks with exptl. characterization, including electrochem., x-ray diffraction, and XPS.  The results demonstrate a series of proton intercalation reaction (β-MnO2 → γ-MnOOH) and two-phase conversion reactions (γ-MnOOH → Mn(OH)2 → λ-MnO2) during the first cycle and Li and H cointercalation in the host structure of λ-MnO2 spinel during the 100th cycle.  It is remarkable that the addn. of Bi2O3 in the nanosized β-MnO2 cathode exhibits outstanding capacity.  After 100 dischargings, the battery demonstrates a capacity of 316 mA h g-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The limited intrinsic cond. of two-dimensional (2D) MoS2 nanosheets compromises its high electrocatalytic performance.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Oxygen vacancies enhance pseudocapacitive charge storage properties of MoO3-x</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Hyung-Seok; Cook, John B.; Lin, Hao; Ko, Jesse S.; Tolbert, Sarah H.; Ozolins, Vidvuds; Dunn, Bruce</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The short charging times and high power capabilities assocd. with capacitive energy storage make this approach an attractive alternative to batteries.  One limitation of electrochem. capacitors is their low energy d. and for this reason, there is widespread interest in pseudocapacitive materials that use Faradaic reactions to store charge.  One candidate pseudocapacitive material is orthorhombic MoO3 (α-MoO3), a layered compd. with a high theor. capacity for lithium (279 mA h g-1 or 1,005 C g-1).  Here, we report on the properties of reduced α-MoO3-x(R-MoO3-x) and compare it with fully oxidized α-MoO3 (F-MoO3).  The introduction of oxygen vacancies leads to a larger interlayer spacing that promotes faster charge storage kinetics and enables the α-MoO3 structure to be retained during the insertion and removal of Li ions.  The higher specific capacity of the R-MoO3-x is attributed to the reversible formation of a significant amt. of Mo4+ following lithiation.  This study underscores the potential importance of incorporating oxygen vacancies into transition metal oxides as a strategy for increasing the charge storage kinetics of redox-active materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref41" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1039/C1CE05700F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tong, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Room-Temperature Ferromagnetism in Hierarchically Branched MoO3 Nanostructures</span>. <i>CrystEngComm</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>14</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">4</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">1419</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1424</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An experimental and computational study to understand the lithium storage mechanism in molybdenum disulfide</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sen, Uttam Kumar; Johari, Priya; Basu, Sohini; Nayak, Chandrani; Mitra, Sagar</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10243-10254</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The lithium storage mechanism in molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) has been comprehensively investigated as the existing conversion-based storage mechanism is unable to explain the reason behind its high practical capacity, high polarization losses, and the change in the discharge profile after the 1st charge-discharge cycle.  To resolve these issues and to gain a deeper understanding of MoS2-based Li-ion batteries, for the first time, we have studied the reaction mechanism of the MoS2 anode using various exptl. techniques such as XRD, Raman spectroscopy, electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, XANES, and EXAFS, as well as ab initio d. functional theory based calcns.  On the basis of the results presented here, and in line with some exptl. findings, we find that the reaction of MoS2 with Li is not as simple as with usual metal oxide based conversion reactions, but that the pathway of the conversion reaction changes after the first discharge process.  In the first discharge process, lithiation is initiated by a limited intercalation process, followed by a conversion reaction that produces molybdenum nanoparticles (Mo) and lithium sulfide (Li2S).  Whereas, unlike oxide-based conversion materials, MoS2 does not transverse back during the delithiation process.  Indeed, instead of MoS2 formation, we identified the presence of polysulfur after the complete cycle.  In consecutive cycles, polysulfur reacts with lithium and forms Li2S/Li2S2, and this Li-S reaction is found to be highly reversible in nature and the only source of the high practical capacity obsd. in this electrode.  To validate our exptl. findings, an at. scale ab initio computational study was also carried out, which likewise suggests that Li first intercalates between the MoS2 layers but that after a certain concn., it reacts with MoS2 to form Li2S.  The calcns. also support the non-reversibility of the conversion reaction, by showing that Mo + Li2S formation is energetically more favorable than the re-formation of MoS2 + Li.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.5b06538"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ryu, W.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wilson, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sohn, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shaulsky, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schroers, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Elimelech, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Taylor, A.                         cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Heterogeneous WSx/WO3 Thorn-Bush Nanofiber Electrodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ryu, Won-Hee; Wilson, Hope; Sohn, Sungwoo; Li, Jinyang; Tong, Xiao; Shaulsky, Evyatar; Schroers, Jan; Elimelech, Menachem; Taylor, Andre D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Heterogeneous electrode materials with hierarchical architectures promise to enable considerable improvement in future energy storage devices.  In this study, we report on a tailored synthetic strategy used to create heterogeneous tungsten sulfide/oxide core-shell nanofiber materials with vertically and randomly aligned thorn-bush features, and we evaluate them as potential anode materials for high-performance Na-ion batteries.  The WSx (2 ≤ x ≤ 3, amorphous WS3 and cryst. WS2) nanofiber is successfully prepd. by electrospinning and subsequent calcination in a reducing atm. We elucidate the evolving morphol. and structural features of the nanofibers during post-thermal treatment.  The heterogeneous thorn-bush nanofiber electrodes deliver a high second discharge capacity of 791 mAh g-1 and improved cycle performance for 100 cycles compared to the pristine WSx nanofiber.         </div><div class="casAbstract">It is demonstrated for the first time that surface passivation of 2D nanosheets of MoS2 by an ultrathin and uniform layer of HfO2 can significantly improve the cyclic performance of sodium ion batteries.  After 50 charge/discharge cycles, bare MoS2 and HfO2 coated MoS2 electrodes deliver the specific capacity of 435 and 636 mAh g-1, resp., at c.d. of 100 mA g-1.  These results imply that batteries using HfO2 coated MoS2 anodes retain 91% of the initial capacity; in contrast, bare MoS2 anodes retain only 63%. Moreover, the amorphous nature of HfO2 allows facile diffusion of Na ions.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Coordination polymer structure and revisited hydrogen evolution catalytic mechanism for amorphous molybdenum sulfide</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tran, Phong D.; Tran, Thu V.; Orio, Maylis; Torelli, Stephane; Truong, Quang Duc; Nayuki, Keiichiro; Sasaki, Yoshikazu; Chiam, Sing Yang; Yi, Ren; Honma, Itaru; Barber, James; Artero, Vincent</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Molybdenum sulfides are very attractive noble-metal-free electrocatalysts for the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) from water.  The at. structure and identity of the catalytically active sites have been well established for cryst. molybdenum disulfide (c-MoS2) but not for amorphous molybdenum sulfide (a-MoSx), which exhibits significantly higher HER activity compared to its cryst. counterpart.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The catalytic activities of sulfur sites in amorphous MoSx for the electrochem. hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) was investigated in aq. 0.5 M H2SO4 electrolyte.  Using XPS and linear sweep voltammetry, we found the turnover frequency for H2 prodn. to increase linearly with the percentage of S atoms with higher electron binding energies.  These S atoms could be apical S2- and/or bridging S22-.  To distinguish the catalytic performances of these two types of atoms, we turn to quantum chem. simulations using d. functional theory.  The apical S2- atoms were found to adsorb H weakly with a Gibbs free energy for at. H adsorption (ΔGH) in excess of +1 eV, and were thus ruled out as reaction sites for HER.  In situ Raman spectroscopy of the model [Mo3S13]2- cluster further demonstrate the higher catalytic reactivity of the bridging S22- over terminal S22- (which have lower electron binding energy) for proton redn.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref48" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1021/ja037666o"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hibble, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Understanding the structure and phase changes assocd. with conversion-type materials is key to optimizing their electrochem. performance in Li-ion batteries.  For example, molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) offers a capacity up to 3-fold higher (∼1 Ah/g) than the currently used graphite anodes, but they suffer from limited Coulombic efficiency and capacity fading.  The lack of insights into the structural dynamics induced by electrochem. conversion of MoS2 still hampers its implementation in high energy-d. batteries.  Here, by combining ab initio d.-functional theory (DFT) simulation with electrochem. anal., we found new sulfur-enriched intermediates that progressively insulate MoS2 electrodes and cause instability from the first discharge cycle.  Because of this, the choice of conductive additives is crit. for the battery performance.  We investigate the mechanistic role of carbon additive by comparing equal loading of std.                         cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrodeposition of copper: the nucleation mechanisms</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grujicic, Darko; Pesic, Batric</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Science Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The nucleation mechanisms of Cu during electrodeposition of thin films from sulfate solns. were studied by using the electrochem. techniques (cyclic voltammetry and chronoamperometry) and at. force microscopy (AFM).  Near atomically smooth glassy C was used as the deposition substrate (electrode).  The Cu nucleation mechanisms were examd. as a function of soln. pH, Cu concn., deposition potential, temp., and background electrolyte.  With pH and Cu concn. increase, the nuclei size increased, while the nuclei population d. decreased.  An increase of deposition potential produced smaller nuclei and higher nuclei population d.  Temp. affected the morphol. of deposited Cu.  The presence of background electrolyte also influenced the morphol. and population d. of Cu nuclei.  The nucleation mechanisms were examd. by fitting the exptl. data (chronoamperometry) into the Scharifker-Hills nucleation models.  At pH 1, in the absence of background electrolyte, Cu nucleation was instantaneous.  At pH 2 and 3, the mechanism was inconclusive.  In the presence of background electrolyte, the mechanism at pH 1 and 2 was mixed, while at pH 3, the mechanism was progressive nucleation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref56" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1088/0022-3727/44/44/443001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oskam, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Radisic, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hoffmann, P. The mechanism of island nucleation and growth dictates the shape, orientation, and no. d. of islands, and ultimately, the structure and properties of thin films.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Core shell MoS2/C nanospheres embedded in foam-like interconnected macroporous carbon sheets composite were successfully fabricated by a facile glucose carbonization process.  In this unique architecture, the interconnected void nanospheres produced by SiO2 nanospheres homogeneously surround the core shell MoS2/C nanospheres.  When evaluated an anode material for sodium-ion batteries, the as-synthesized porous carbon sheets supported core shell MoS2/C composite displays a discharge capacity of 523 mAh g-1 after 100 discharge/charge cycles at the c.d. of 0.1 A g-1.                         cas_cit59R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">59</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hydrogen bond-assisted synthesis of MoS2/reduced graphene oxide composite with excellent electrochemical performances for lithium and sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qin, Weiwei; Li, Yuan; Teng, Yongqiang; Qin, Tao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Colloid and Interface Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">MoS2/reduced graphene oxide composites (MoS2/rGO) were successfully prepd. by a designed tris(hydroxymethyl)methyl aminomethane (named THAM)-assisted hydrothermal method, which involves the modification of THAM on the surfaces of graphene oxide via hydrogen bonds and then the adsorption of MoO2-4 on the decorated surfaces due to the electrostatic attraction.  The three-dimensional framework of interconnected rGO nanosheets provides good electronic cond. and facile strain release during the electrochem. reaction, thus enhancing the overall performance of the MoS2-based electrode.  Herein, the composite delivers high specific capacity, excellent cycling stability and rate performance for lithium- and sodium- ions batteries (LIBs and SIBs).  The MoS2/rGO anode exhibits capacities of 880 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1 after 200 cycles and 396 mAh g-1 even at 2 A g-1 after 2000 cycles for LIBs.  As to SIBs, the reversible capacities of 485 mAh g-1 and 339 mAh g-1 can be retained at 0.1 A g-1 after 60 cycles and 0.5 A g-1 after 300 cycles, resp.  Our results demonstrate that the MoS2/rGO anode is one of the attractive anodes for LIBs and SIBs.  Furthermore, the facile method can be extended to biosensing, catalytic, and biomedical applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref60" class="refNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit60" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2017.09.088"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rational Design of MoS2-Reduced Graphene Oxide Sponges as Free-Standing Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. J.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>332</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">260</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">266</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.cej.2017.09.088</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D332%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D260-266%26journal%3DChem.%2BEng.%2BJ.%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DW.%2BQin%26author%3DJ.%2BXie%26author%3DR.%2BLin%26author%3DZ.%2BWang%26author%3DL.%2BPan%26author%3DW.%2BMai%26title%3DRational%2BDesign%2Bof%2BMoS2-Reduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2BSponges%2Bas%2BFree-Standing%2BAnodes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.cej.2017.09.088&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b22452&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.cej.2017.09.088&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rational design of MoS2-reduced graphene oxide sponges as free-standing anodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Jinliang; Qin, Wei; Xie, Junpeng; Lin, Rui; Wang, Zilong; Pan, Likun; Mai, Wenjie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">332</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">260-266</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Currently, the search for high capacity, low cost and free-standing electrodes for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is one of the major challenges in energy storage field.  In this work, we rationally design MoS2-reduced graphene oxide (MS-RGO) sponges via a simple freeze-drying of ammonium tetrathiomolybdate-graphene oxide mixed soln. and a subsequent thermal treatment in N2/H2 atmosphere, and employ these sponges as free-standing anodes for SIBs.  The MS-RGO sponges exhibit a porous conducive structure that can facilitate the charge transport and thus show an excellent electrochem. performance.  The free-standing sponge electrodes display a maximal reversible specific capacity of 372.0 mAh g-1 (0.49 mAh cm-2) at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 50 cycles.  Even at a high c.d. of 1 A g-1, a capacity of 192.2 mAh g-1 (0.25 mAh cm-2) is maintained after 345 cycles.  The results show that MS-RGO sponges are promising free-standing electrode materials for rechargeable SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188ae04b72075e-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48'],'ref49':['cit49'],'ref50':['cit50'],'ref51':['cit51'],'ref52':['cit52'],'ref53':['cit53'],'ref54':['cit54'],'ref55':['cit55'],'ref56':['cit56'],'ref57':['cit57'],'ref58':['cit58'],'ref59':['cit59'],'ref60':['cit60']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">38</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">20304</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">20315</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C7TA06469A</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">One-step synthesis of multilayered 2D Sn nanodendrites as a high-performance anode material for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Tae-Hee; Hong, Kyung-Sik; Sohn, DongRak; Kim, MinJoong; Nam, Do-Hwan; Cho, EunAe; Kwon, HyukSang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">38</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">20304-20315</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Multilayered 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites were synthesized by a 1-step electrodeposition process from an aq. soln. contg. coumarin (C9H6O2).  Under a high cathodic c.d. that is necessary for dendritic growth, H bubbles evolved with Sn redn.  The H bubbles limited the diffusion path of the Sn ions to space between H bubbles and concealed the splitting sites for addnl. branches, which suppressed the development of the branches.  The addn. of coumarin greatly reduced the departure diam. of the H bubbles and thus made the growth of the Sn dendrites with a high d. of nanosized branches possible.  Also, coumarin suppressed Sn crystallite growth along the [001] direction, promoting 2-dimensional growth of the 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites along the set of [220] directions that are normal to the [001] direction.  These 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites grew layer-by-layer on a substrate, forming a unique multilayered structure.  As an anode material for Na-ion batteries, the multilayered 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites exhibited a high specific capacity of 783.88 mA h g-1 and excellent cycle performance with 96.6% capacity retention over 300 cycles.  Also, the multilayered 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites showed a superior rate capability of 412.84 mA g-1 at 5C.  The outstanding performance is attributed to a high d. of free space inside the multilayered 2-dimensional Sn nanodendrites and the nanosized Sn branches that offer short diffusion paths for Na ions.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1021/nn505536t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nam, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, T.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, K.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwon, H.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Template-Free Electrochemical Synthesis of Sn Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode Materials for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">11</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">11824</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">11835</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/nn505536t</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Template-Free Electrochemical Synthesis of Sn Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode Materials for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nam, Do-Hwan; Kim, Tae-Hee; Hong, Kyung-Sik; Kwon, Hyuk-Sang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11824-11835</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn nanofibers with a high aspect ratio are successfully synthesized using a simple electrodeposition process from an aq. soln. without the use of templates.  The synthesis approach involves the rapid electrochem. deposition of Sn accompanied by the strong adsorption of Triton X-100, which can function as a growth modifier for the Sn crystallites.  Triton X-100 is adsorbed on the {200} crystallog. planes of Sn in an elongated configuration and suppressed the preferential growth of Sn along the [100] direction.  Consequently, the Sn electrodeposits are forced to grow anisotropically in a direction normal to the (112) or (‾112) plane, forming one-dimensional nanofibers.  As electrode materials for the Na-ion batteries, the Sn nanofibers exhibit a high reversible capacity and an excellent cycle performance; the charge capacity is maintained at 776.26 mA-h/g after 100 cycles, which corresponds to a retention of 95.09% of the initial charge capacity.  The superior electrochem. performance of the Sn nanofibers is mainly attributed to the high mech. stability of the nanofibers, which originate from highly anisotropic expansion during sodiation and the pore vols. existing between the nanofibers.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1021/jp504055j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nam, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, K.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lim, S.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, T.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwon, H.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Properties of Electrodeposited Sn Anodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Phys. Chem. C</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>118</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">35</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">20086</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">20093</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/jp504055j</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Properties of Electrodeposited Sn Anodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nam, Do-Hwan; Hong, Kyung-Sik; Lim, Sung-Jin; Kim, Tae-Hee; Kwon, Hyuk-Sang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">118</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">35</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">20086-20093</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1932-7447</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. properties and sodiation/desodiation mechanism of Sn electrodes prepd. by electrodeposition were investigated for Na-ion batteries.  The sodiation of the electrodeposited Sn electrodes proceeded via three conjugated reactions in the cutoff voltage range of 0.001-0.65 V, as follows: the β-Sn phase, which contains a small amt. of Na, was sodiated into amorphous NaSn, and cryst. Na9Sn4 and Na15Sn4 were then formed sequentially.  However, the cycle performance of the electrode was highly dependent on the morphologies and the crystallog. orientations of the electrodeposits.  The Sn electrode composed of coarse particles had the poor cycle performance due to the elec. isolation of Sn caused by the large vol. changes during cycling.  In contrast, the thin-layered Sn electrode exhibited a stable cycle performance in which the charge capacity was maintained at 607.51 mA-h/g after 40 cycles, which corresponds to 98.21% of its initial charge capacity.  The improvement in the cyclability of the layered Sn electrode was attributed not only to reduced structural degrdns. by restricting the cutoff voltage but also to an improved bond between the Sn electrodeposit and the Cu substrate.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b04225"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, K.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nam, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lim, S.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sohn, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, T.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwon, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemically Synthesized Sb/Sb2O3 Composites as High-Capacity Anode Materials Utilizing a Reversible Conversion Reaction for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Mater.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemically Synthesized Sb/Sb2O3 Composites as High-Capacity Anode Materials Utilizing a Reversible Conversion Reaction for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Kyung-Sik; Nam, Do-Hwan; Lim, Sung-Jin; Sohn, DongRak; Kim, Tae-Hee; Kwon, HyukSang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb/Sb2O3 composites are synthesized by a one-step electrodeposition process from an aq. electrolytic bath contg. a potassium antimony tartrate complex.  The synthesis process involves the electrodeposition of Sb simultaneously with the chem. deposition of Sb2O3, which allows for the direct deposition of morula-like Sb/Sb2O3 particles on the current collector without using a binder.  Structural characterization confirms that the Sb/Sb2O3 composites are composed of approx. 90 mol % metallic Sb and 10 mol % cryst. Sb2O3.  The composite exhibits a high reversible capacity (670 mAh/g) that is higher than the theor. capacity of Sb (660 mAh/g).  The high reversible capacity results from the conversion reaction between Na2O and Sb2O3 that occurs addnl. to the alloying/dealloying reaction of Sb with Na.  Moreover, the Sb/Sb2O3 composite shows excellent cycle performance with 91.8% capacity retention over 100 cycles, and a superior rate capability of 212 mAh/g at a high c.d. of 3300 mA/g.  The outstanding cycle performance is attributed to an amorphous Na2O phase generated by the conversion reaction, which inhibits agglomeration of Sb particles and acts as an effective buffer against vol. change of Sb during cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2017.04.017"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Teng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Świerczek, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">MoS2 Nanosheets Vertically Grown on Reduced Graphene Oxide via Oxygen Bonds with Carbon Coating as Ultrafast Sodium Ion Batteries Anodes</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>119</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">91</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">100</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rational material design is a key to develop high performance electrode for Na ion batteries (SIBs).  Sandwich-like graphene@MoS2@C sheets (G@MS@C), with MoS2 nanosheets perpendicularly connecting with reduced graphene oxide (rGO) through the direct chem. coupling (C-O-Mo bonds), are synthesized by a facile two-step method, which involves in situ growth of MoS2 on rGO sheets and a followed amorphous carbon coverage process.  The interfacial interaction via the C-O-Mo bonds can accelerate electron transport rate and enhance structural stability of G@MS@C electrode.  Meanwhile, the vertical nanostructure provides more active sites and short diffusion distance for sodium ion reaction, leading to fast electrode reaction kinetics.  The rGO sheets and carbon shells not only improve the elec. cond. of the composite, but also act as buffers to accommodate the vol. changes of MoS2 and ensure the integrity of the electrode during cycling.  As an anode material for SIBs, the G@MS@C electrode shows superior reversible capacity (520 mAh/g after 110 cycles at 100 mA/g), excellent rate capability (304 mAh/g after 200 cycles at 5 A/g) and stable cycling performance (260 mAh/g after 300 cycles at 10 A/g).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1021/nl404165c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kravchyk, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Walter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kovalenko, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report colloidal synthesis of Sb nanocrystals with mean size tunable in the 10-20 nm range and with narrow size distributions of 7-11%.  In comparison to microcryst. Sb, 10 and 20 nm Sb nanocrystals exhibit enhanced rate capability and higher cycling stability as anode materials in rechargeable Li-ion and Na-ion batteries.  All three particle sizes of Sb have high and similar Li-ion and Na-ion charge storage capacities of 580-640 mA-h/g at moderate charging/discharging current densities of 0.5-1C (1C rate is 660 mA/g).  At all C-rates (0.5-20C, e.g. current densities of 0.33-13.2 A/g), capacities of 20 nm Sb particles are systematically better than for both 10 nm and bulk Sb.  At 20C rate, retention of charge storage capacities by 10 and 20 nm Sb nanocrystals can reach 78-85% of the low-rate value, indicating that rate capability of Sb nanostructures can be comparable to the best Li-ion intercalation anodes and is so far unprecedented for Na-ion storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1021/ja310347x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Darwiche, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Marino, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sougrati, M.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pure micrometric antimony can be successfully used as neg. electrode material in Na-ion batteries, sustaining a capacity close to 600 mAh g-1 at a high rate with a Coulombic efficiency of 99 over 160 cycles, an extremely high capacity compared to any other compd. tested against both Li and Na.  The reaction mechanism with Na does not simply go through the alloying mechanism obsd. for Li where the intermediate species are those expected from the phase diagram.  In the case of Na, the intermediate phases are mostly amorphous and could not be precisely identified.  Surprisingly, it is evidenced that a competition takes place at the end of the discharge of the Sb/Na cell between the formation of the hexagonal and the cubic polymorphs of Na3Sb, the last being described in the literature as unstable at atm. pressure and only synthesized under high pressure (1-9 GPa).         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions.  Understanding of the reaction mechanism between Sn and Na ions will provide insight towards exploring and designing new alloy-based anode materials for Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1039/c2cc17129e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kovarik, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nie, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Capacity, Reversible Alloying Reactions in SnSb/C Nanocomposites for Na-Ion Battery Applications</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Liq. plasma, produced by nanosecond pulses, provides an efficient and simple way to fabricate a nanocomposite architecture of Co3O4/CNTs from carbon nanotubes (CNTs) and clusters of Co3O4 nanoparticles in deionized water.  The crucial feature of the composite's structure is that Co3O4 nanoparticle clusters are uniformly dispersed and anchored to CNT networks in which Co3O4 guarantees high electrochem. reactivity towards sodium, and CNTs provide cond. and stabilize the anode structure. Unfortunately, most reported metal phosphides consist of irregular particles ranged from several hundreds nanometers to tens of micrometers, thus delivering limited cyclic stability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Mesoporous single crystal (PSC) oxides have been reported as presenting higher electrochem. performances than bulk materials in lithium ion batteries operating via intercalation processes.  Here, we extend this study to the electrochem. behavior of mesoporous Cr2O3 vs. Li+/Li0.  We confirm that the Cr2O3 reacts towards Li through a conversion reaction mechanism leading, upon discharge, to the formation of large metallic chromium nanoparticles (10 nm); the latter are embedded into a Li2O matrix together with, in this specific case, a copious amt. of polymeric materials coming from electrolyte degrdn., surrounding the particles, and filling the pores.  During the following charge, re-oxidn. of the nanoparticles occurs with the formation of CrO1-x, with the main difference, as opposed to bulk Cr2O3 electrodes, being the preservation of the polymeric layer at the end of the charge.  We believe the material mesoporosity, via capillary effects, to be at the origin of such a difference.  These electrolyte degrdn. products are shown to help in maintaining the material mesoporosity for a great no. of cycles; and interestingly they are not detrimental to the cell performance in terms of capacity retention while presenting great advantages in terms of charge transfer by reducing diffusion lengths, namely for Li+ ions. The authors studied the conversion reaction of binary metal fluorides, FeF2 and CuF2, using local and bulk probes to better understand the mechanisms underlying their contrasting electrochem. behavior.  X-ray pair-distribution-function and magnetization measurements were used to det. changes in short-range ordering, particle size and microstructure, while high-resoln. TEM and EELS were used to measure the at.-level structure of individual particles and map the phase distribution in the initial and fully lithiated electrodes.  Both FeF2 and CuF2 react with lithium via a direct conversion process with no intercalation step, but there are differences in the conversion process and final phase distribution.  During the reaction of Li+ with FeF2, small metallic iron nanoparticles ( &lt; 5 nm in diam.) nucleate in close proximity to the converted LiF phase, as a result of the low diffusivity of iron.  The iron nanoparticles are interconnected and form a bicontinuous network, which provides a pathway for local electron transport through the insulating LiF phase.  The massive interface formed between nanoscale solid phases provides a pathway for ionic transport during the conversion process.  These results offer the 1st exptl. evidence explaining the origins of the high lithium reversibility in FeF2.  In contrast to FeF2, no continuous Cu network was obsd. in the lithiated CuF2; rather, the converted Cu segregates to large particles (5-12 nm in diam.) during the 1st discharge, which may be partially responsible for the lack of reversibility in the CuF2 electrode.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref20" class="rightTabRefNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit20" data-doi="10.1007/s12274-016-1365-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Conversion materials for lithium ion batteries have recently attracted considerable attention due to their exceptional specific capacities.  Some metal fluorides, such as CuF2, are promising candidates for cathode materials owing to their high operating potential, which stems from the high electronegativity of fluorine.  However, the high ionicity of the metal-fluorine bond leads to a large band gap that renders these materials poor electronic conductors.  Nanosizing the active material and embedding it within a conductive matrix such as carbon can greatly improve its electrochem. performance.  In contrast to other fluorides, such as FeF2 and NiF2, good capacity retention has not, however, been achieved for CuF2.  The reaction mechanisms that occur in the first and subsequent cycles and the reasons for the poor charge performance of CuF2 are studied in this paper via a variety of characterization methods.  In situ pair distribution function anal. clearly shows CuF2 conversion in the first discharge.  However, few structural changes are seen in the following charge and subsequent cycles.  Cyclic voltammetry results, in combination with in situ X-ray absorption near edge structure and ex situ NMR spectroscopy, indicate that Cu dissoln. is assocd. with the consumption of the LiF phase, which occurs during the first charge via the formation of a Cu1+ intermediate.  The dissoln. process consequently prevents Cu and LiF from transforming back to CuF2.  Such side reactions result in negligible capacity in subsequent cycles and make this material challenging to use in a rechargeable battery.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref22" class="rightTabRefNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit22" data-doi="10.1016/j.mattod.2014.10.040"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nitta, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.
