        </div><div class="casAbstract">The structural transformations that occur when FeF3 is cycled at room temp. in a Li cell were investigated using a combination of x-ray diffraction (XRD), pair distribution function (PDF) anal., and magic-angle-spinning NMR spectroscopy.  Two regions are seen on discharge.  The first occurs between Li = 0 and 1.0 and involves an insertion reaction.  This first region actually comprises two steps.  First, a two-phase reaction between Li = 0 and 0.5 occurs, and the Li0.5FeF3 phase that is formed gives rise to a Li NMR resonance due to Li+ ions near both Fe3+ and Fe2+ ions.  On the basis of the PDF data, the local structure of this phase is closer to the rutile structure than the original ReO3 structure.  Second, a single-phase intercalation reaction occurs between Li = 0.5 and 1.0, for which the Li NMR data indicate a progressive increase in the concn. of Fe2+ ions.  In the second region, the conversion reaction, superparamagnetic, nanosized (∼3 nm) Fe metal is formed, as indicated by the XRD and NMR data, along with some LiF and a third phase that is rich in Li and F.  The charge process involves the formation of a series of intercalation phases with increasing Fe oxidn. state, which, on the basis of the Li NMR and PDF data, have local structures that are similar to the intercalation phases seen during the first stage of the discharge process.  The solid-state NMR and XRD results for the rutile phase FeF2 are presented for comparison, and the data indicate that an insertion reaction also occurs, which is accompanied by the formation of LiF.  This is followed by the formation of Fe nanoparticles and LiF via a conversion reaction.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.7b11685"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The short charging times and high power capabilities assocd. with capacitive energy storage make this approach an attractive alternative to batteries. One candidate pseudocapacitive material is orthorhombic MoO3 (α-MoO3), a layered compd. with a high theor. capacity for lithium (279 mA h g-1 or 1,005 C g-1).  Here, we report on the properties of reduced α-MoO3-x(R-MoO3-x) and compare it with fully oxidized α-MoO3 (F-MoO3). The higher specific capacity of the R-MoO3-x is attributed to the reversible formation of a significant amt. of Mo4+ following lithiation.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The lithium storage mechanism in molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) has been comprehensively investigated as the existing conversion-based storage mechanism is unable to explain the reason behind its high practical capacity, high polarization losses, and the change in the discharge profile after the 1st charge-discharge cycle.  To resolve these issues and to gain a deeper understanding of MoS2-based Li-ion batteries, for the first time, we have studied the reaction mechanism of the MoS2 anode using various exptl. techniques such as XRD, Raman spectroscopy, electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, XANES, and EXAFS, as well as ab initio d. functional theory based calcns.  On the basis of the results presented here, and in line with some exptl. findings, we find that the reaction of MoS2 with Li is not as simple as with usual metal oxide based conversion reactions, but that the pathway of the conversion reaction changes after the first discharge process.  In the first discharge process, lithiation is initiated by a limited intercalation process, followed by a conversion reaction that produces molybdenum nanoparticles (Mo) and lithium sulfide (Li2S).  Whereas, unlike oxide-based conversion materials, MoS2 does not transverse back during the delithiation process.  Indeed, instead of MoS2 formation, we identified the presence of polysulfur after the complete cycle.  In consecutive cycles, polysulfur reacts with lithium and forms Li2S/Li2S2, and this Li-S reaction is found to be highly reversible in nature and the only source of the high practical capacity obsd. in this electrode.  To validate our exptl. findings, an at. scale ab initio computational study was also carried out, which likewise suggests that Li first intercalates between the MoS2 layers but that after a certain concn., it reacts with MoS2 to form Li2S.  The calcns. also support the non-reversibility of the conversion reaction, by showing that Mo + Li2S formation is energetically more favorable than the re-formation of MoS2 + Li.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.5b06538"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ryu, W.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wilson, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sohn, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shaulsky, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schroers, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Elimelech, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Taylor, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">It is demonstrated for the first time that surface passivation of 2D nanosheets of MoS2 by an ultrathin and uniform layer of HfO2 can significantly improve the cyclic performance of sodium ion batteries.  After 50 charge/discharge cycles, bare MoS2 and HfO2 coated MoS2 electrodes deliver the specific capacity of 435 and 636 mAh g-1, resp., at c.d. of 100 mA g-1.  These results imply that batteries using HfO2 coated MoS2 anodes retain 91% of the initial capacity; in contrast, bare MoS2 anodes retain only 63%.  Also, HfO2 coated MoS2 anodes show one of the highest reported capacity values for MoS2.  Cyclic voltammetry and XPS results suggest that HfO2 does not take part in electrochem. reaction.  The mechanism of capacity retention with HfO2 coating is explained by ex situ transmission electron microscope imaging and elec. impedance spectroscopy.  HfO2 acts as a passivation layer at the anode/electrolyte interface and prevents structural degrdn. during charge/discharge process.  Moreover, the amorphous nature of HfO2 allows facile diffusion of Na ions.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Molybdenum sulfides are very attractive noble-metal-free electrocatalysts for the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) from water.  The at. structure and identity of the catalytically active sites have been well established for cryst. molybdenum disulfide (c-MoS2) but not for amorphous molybdenum sulfide (a-MoSx), which exhibits significantly higher HER activity compared to its cryst. counterpart.  Here it is shown that HER-active a-MoSx, prepd. either as nanoparticles or as films, is a mol.-based coordination polymer consisting of discrete [Mo3S13]2- building blocks.  Of the three terminal disulfide (S22-) ligands within these clusters, two are shared to form the polymer chain.  The third one remains free and generates molybdenum hydride moieties as the active site under H2 evolution conditions.  Such a mol. structure therefore provides a basis for revisiting the mechanism of a-MoSx catalytic activity, as well as explaining some of its special properties such as reductive activation and corrosion.  The findings open up new avenues for the rational optimization of this HER electrocatalyst as an alternative to platinum.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref46" class="rightTabRefNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit46" data-doi="10.1038/s41598-017-16015-y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhai, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tuning the Composition and Structure of Amorphous Molybdenum Sulfide/Carbon Black Nanocomposites by Radiation Technique for Highly Efficient Hydrogen Evolution</span>. <i>Sci.                 10.1002/adfm.201102669</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reversible Lithium Storage with High Mobility at Structural Defects in Amorphous Molybdenum Dioxide Electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ku, Jun H.; Ryu, Ji Heon; Kim, Sun Ha; Han, Oc Hee; Oh, Seung M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">22</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3658-3664, S3658/1-S3658/5</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">This work demonstrates that structural defects in amorphous metal oxide electrodes can serve as a reversible Li+ storage site for lithium secondary batteries.  For instance, molybdenum dioxide electrode in amorphous form (a-MoO2) exhibits an unexpectedly high Li+ storage capacity (up to four Li per MoO2 unit), which is larger by a factor of four than that for the cryst. counterpart.  The conversion-type lithiation is discarded for this electrode from the absence of Mo metal and lithium oxide in the lithiated a-MoO2 electrode and the retention of local structural framework.  The sloping voltage profile in a wide potential range suggests that Li+ ions are inserted into the structural defects that are electrochem. nonequivalent.  This electrode also shows an excellent cycle stability and rate capability.  The latter feature is seemingly due to a rather opened Li+ diffusion pathway provided by the structural defects.  A high Li+ mobility is confirmed from NMR study.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref53" class="rightTabRefNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit53" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA03788J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Uchaker, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Y. Z.; Li, S.; Candelaria, S. L.; Hu, S.; Cao, G. Z.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous and nanocryst. V2O5 were prepd. through a combination of sol-gel processing paired with electrochem. deposition and studied as cathodes for Na-ion batteries.  Amorphous V2O5 demonstrated superior electrochem. properties upon sodiation as compared to its cryst. counterpart.  More specifically, amorphous V pentoxide had a measured capacity of 241 mA h g-1, twice the capacity of its cryst. contemporary at 120 mA h g-1.  The amorphous V pentoxide demonstrated a much higher discharge potential, energy d., and cycle stability.  The development of amorphous materials could enable the usage and design of previously unexplored electrode materials; herein, the possible relation between the improved sodiation properties and the amorphous structure is discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref54" class="rightTabRefNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit54"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wyckoff, R.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref55" class="rightTabRefNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit55" data-doi="10.1016/S0013-4686(02)00161-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grujicic, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pesic, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrodeposition of Copper: The Nucleation Mechanisms</span>. <i>Electrochim.                         rightTab-cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrodeposition of copper: the nucleation mechanisms</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grujicic, Darko; Pesic, Batric</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2002</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">47</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Science Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The nucleation mechanisms of Cu during electrodeposition of thin films from sulfate solns. were studied by using the electrochem. techniques (cyclic voltammetry and chronoamperometry) and at. force microscopy (AFM).  Near atomically smooth glassy C was used as the deposition substrate (electrode).  The Cu nucleation mechanisms were examd. as a function of soln. pH, Cu concn., deposition potential, temp., and background electrolyte.  With pH and Cu concn. increase, the nuclei size increased, while the nuclei population d. decreased.  An increase of deposition potential produced smaller nuclei and higher nuclei population d.  Temp. affected the morphol. of deposited Cu.  The presence of background electrolyte also influenced the morphol. and population d. of Cu nuclei.  The nucleation mechanisms were examd. by fitting the exptl. data (chronoamperometry) into the Scharifker-Hills nucleation models.  At pH 1, in the absence of background electrolyte, Cu nucleation was instantaneous.  At pH 2 and 3, the mechanism was inconclusive.  In the presence of background electrolyte, the mechanism at pH 1 and 2 was mixed, while at pH 3, the mechanism was progressive nucleation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref56" class="rightTabRefNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit56" data-doi="10.1088/0022-3727/44/44/443001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oskam, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Radisic, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hoffmann, P. M</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Searson, P. C</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Island Growth In Electrodeposition</span>. <i>J. Phys. D: Appl.                 10.1088/0022-3727/44/44/443001</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Island growth in electrodeposition</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Lian; Oskam, Gerko; Radisic, Aleksandar; Hoffmann, Peter M.; Searson, Peter C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physics D: Applied Physics</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">44</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Institute of Physics Publishing</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Electrochem. deposition of metals onto foreign substrates usually occurs through Volmer-Weber island growth.  The mechanism of island nucleation and growth dictates the shape, orientation, and no. d. of islands, and ultimately, the structure and properties of thin films.  With increasing emphasis on deposition of ultrathin films and nanostructures, it is critically important to understand the kinetics of nucleation and growth.  Here a comprehensive review of island growth in electrodeposition is provided and methods for mechanistic anal. in both the kinetic- and diffusion-limited regimes are summarized.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref57" class="rightTabRefNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit57" data-doi="10.1007/s10008-007-0264-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Levi, M.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref58" class="rightTabRefNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit58" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2016.10.073"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Core Shell MoS2/C Nanospheres Embedded in Foam-Like Carbon Sheets Composite with an Interconnected Macroporous Structure as Stable and High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                 10.1016/j.cej.2016.10.073</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Core shell MoS2/C nanospheres embedded in foam-like carbon sheets composite with an interconnected macroporous structure as stable and high-capacity anodes for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Beibei; Xia, Yuan; Wang, Gang; Zhou, Yixuan; Wang, Hui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">309</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">417-425</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Core shell MoS2/C nanospheres embedded in foam-like interconnected macroporous carbon sheets composite were successfully fabricated by a facile glucose carbonization process.  In this unique architecture, the interconnected void nanospheres produced by SiO2 nanospheres homogeneously surround the core shell MoS2/C nanospheres.  When evaluated an anode material for sodium-ion batteries, the as-synthesized porous carbon sheets supported core shell MoS2/C composite displays a discharge capacity of 523 mAh g-1 after 100 discharge/charge cycles at the c.d. of 0.1 A g-1.  Moreover, the 300th discharge capacity at a higher c.d. of 1 A g-1 is 337 mAh g-1.  The superior electrochem. performance of the porous carbon sheets supported core shell MoS2/C composite can be attributed to the supporting carbon sheets with an interconnected macroporous structure, uniform distribution of carbon coated MoS2 nanospheres in carbon matrix, high electronic cond., and thus leading to enhanced structural stability and cyclability of the anode material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref59"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref59" class="rightTabRefNumLink">59</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit59" data-doi="10.1016/j.jcis.2017.10.106"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Teng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hydrogen Bond-Assisted Synthesis of MoS2/Reduced Graphene Oxide Composite with Excellent Electrochemical Performances for Lithium and Sodium Storage</span>. <i>J.                 10.1016/j.jcis.2017.10.106</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit59R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">59</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hydrogen bond-assisted synthesis of MoS2/reduced graphene oxide composite with excellent electrochemical performances for lithium and sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qin, Weiwei; Li, Yuan; Teng, Yongqiang; Qin, Tao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Colloid and Interface Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">512</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">826-833</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCISA5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0021-9797</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">MoS2/reduced graphene oxide composites (MoS2/rGO) were successfully prepd. by a designed tris(hydroxymethyl)methyl aminomethane (named THAM)-assisted hydrothermal method, which involves the modification of THAM on the surfaces of graphene oxide via hydrogen bonds and then the adsorption of MoO2-4 on the decorated surfaces due to the electrostatic attraction.  The three-dimensional framework of interconnected rGO nanosheets provides good electronic cond. and facile strain release during the electrochem. reaction, thus enhancing the overall performance of the MoS2-based electrode.  Herein, the composite delivers high specific capacity, excellent cycling stability and rate performance for lithium- and sodium- ions batteries (LIBs and SIBs).  The MoS2/rGO anode exhibits capacities of 880 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1 after 200 cycles and 396 mAh g-1 even at 2 A g-1 after 2000 cycles for LIBs.  As to SIBs, the reversible capacities of 485 mAh g-1 and 339 mAh g-1 can be retained at 0.1 A g-1 after 60 cycles and 0.5 A g-1 after 300 cycles, resp.  Our results demonstrate that the MoS2/rGO anode is one of the attractive anodes for LIBs and SIBs.  Furthermore, the facile method can be extended to biosensing, catalytic, and biomedical applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref60" class="rightTabRefNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit60" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2017.09.088"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rational Design of MoS2-Reduced Graphene Oxide Sponges as Free-Standing Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Eng. J.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>332</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">260</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">266</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.cej.2017.09.088</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rational design of MoS2-reduced graphene oxide sponges as free-standing anodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Jinliang; Qin, Wei; Xie, Junpeng; Lin, Rui; Wang, Zilong; Pan, Likun; Mai, Wenjie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">332</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">260-266</span>CODEN:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Currently, the search for high capacity, low cost and free-standing electrodes for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is one of the major challenges in energy storage field.  In this work, we rationally design MoS2-reduced graphene oxide (MS-RGO) sponges via a simple freeze-drying of ammonium tetrathiomolybdate-graphene oxide mixed soln. and a subsequent thermal treatment in N2/H2 atmosphere, and employ these sponges as free-standing anodes for SIBs.  The MS-RGO sponges exhibit a porous conducive structure that can facilitate the charge transport and thus show an excellent electrochem. performance.  The free-standing sponge electrodes display a maximal reversible specific capacity of 372.0 mAh g-1 (0.49 mAh cm-2) at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 50 cycles.  Even at a high c.d. of 1 A g-1, a capacity of 192.2 mAh g-1 (0.25 mAh cm-2) is maintained after 345 cycles.  The results show that MS-RGO sponges are promising free-standing electrode materials for rechargeable SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i9">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b22452?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b22452</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images, XRD patterns, cycle performance and Coulombic efficiency, EIS plots, differential capacity plots, charge capacity, and charge–discharge curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22452/suppl_file/am9b22452_si_001.pdf">am9b22452_si_001.pdf (1.36 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="93188ae04b72075e-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

    }());

// Moving the popup before the header content for tab accessibility

<script nonce="93188ae04b72075e-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>
<link rel="stylesheet" type="text/css" href="/pb-assets/smartgroup_ads/Modal-LeadGen-1691422642793.css">
            
        </div>

<script nonce="93188ae04b72075e-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      var emailValid = "",

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();

        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());

        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...

          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");

          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

        <script nonce="93188ae04b72075e-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
